0307887898|5.0|This book is a must have for anyone interested in building a sustainable business in the 21st century.  I know that this is a bold statement but I fear the words \\"startup\\" and \\"entrepreneur\\"  will put off those who don't label themselves as entrepreneurs or think of themselves as working in a startup.      The Lean Startup is a field guide for businesses that need to navigate \\"under conditions of extreme uncertainty.\\"     This increasingly describes the challenges facing large, established organizations that operate in complex, rapidly changing markets, with competitors that can come out of nowhere.  In the face of such uncertainty you are essentially working in startup conditions where the winners are those who can build adaptive organizations that learn faster.  Taken in this light, anyone managing a business must have an entrepreneur's mindset and learn the science of innovation.  This is where The Lean Startup comes in...    Rather than take the theoretical approach of, say, The Fifth Discipline,  Ries has put together a methodical book full of practical ways to embed validated learning and scientific rigor into product and service development.  Of particular value for me was the concept of the MVP - minimum viable product -  that allows you to reframe your product in its most essential (and testable) form and how to establish metrics that matter (no more \\"vanity metrics\\"!).    I highly recommend this book.

0452286751|4.0|This novel stands as a monument to human accomplishment. To each their own but in my opinion a better character has never been brought to life than the protagonist of the Fountainhead, one Howard Roark. Throughout the novel he attacks his goals with a doggedness that is found in only the most disciplined of persons. I could write a short story describing Roark and his personal qualities, however, In this review I would like to focus more heavily on the theme of the novel and Rand's unique philosophy. Suffice to say Howard Roark endures what would have broken ten lesser men and at the conclusion of the novel we find him literally standing on top of the world.<br /><br />As I am sure you know Rand's philosophy is Objectivism. I first fell in love with this unique philosophy around the age of 18 when I was finishing high school. I read the Fountainhead and believed that I had uncovered the solution to all of mankind's problems. Every page seemed to be a personal revelation that would better my life. I tend to be anti-social and individualistic by nature so needless to say I was instantly attracted to a main character who seems oblivious to the outside world and its influences.<br /><br />I am now nineteen and have spent a year in college. I have come to the conclusion that it SUCKS to be alone. It really sucks... Which is in essence what Objectivism preaches. Howard Roark seems to do it without effort in the book, but he is a fictional character and this is real life. I am trying to think of how to summarize the Fountainhead in a way that would make sense to a person who has not read it, however I am not that talented nor devoted to writing yet. Suffice to say if you followed its advice word for word one would be living in a very isolated world. I love being with people and often find the most happiness when I am with them. Think about a few of your most remembered moments in life and ask yourself if you were alone or with other people. That answer is easy for me.<br /><br />I guess what I am really trying to say is that this book lured me in with its idealistic promises but after voraciously reading and re-reading all of Rand's work and having some months to really think it over and apply it to my life, I would just as soon use it as kindling as anything else. With some 1000 reviews before me I doubt anyone will even read this, at least not for a while. I am ok with that because I am writing this review more for my purpose than any other, but there truly is some sense to what I have written.<br /><br />In closing, this book is wonderfully written, the main character Howard Roark is incredible, a shining example of human achievement, but keep my above mentioned thoughts in mind when and if you read this book. I believe that some aspects of Objectivism like judging people by the quality of their work are excellent concepts but as a whole, I would rather spend my time with imperfect people (LIKE ME, Nobodies perfect) than to live an isolated life striving for a perfect ideal.<br /><br />One final thought, if you REALLY still want to get a true grip of Rand's philosophy it is essential to read Atlas Shrugged. The Fountainhead is essentially just a broad outline of her philosophy while the voluminous Atlas Shrugged is the core document, ground zero if you will.<br /><br />Samam really trying to say is that this book lured me in with its idealistic promises but after voraciously reading and re-reading all of Rand's work and having some months to really think it over and apply it to my life, I would just as soon use it as kindling as anything else. With some 1000 reviews before me I doubt anyone will even read this, at least not for a while. I am ok with that because I am writing this review more for my purpose than any other, but there truly is some sense to what I have written. B003UBTX72|5.0|Life by Keith Richards starts out like your typical Rock-n-Roll autobiography - getting stopped by the cops with a lot of drugs in the car, wondering if he and his friends are going to get busted.<br /><br />If that was what this entire book was about, it would be a total waste of time.  I mean, you can turn on TV and get the same thing for free.  But thankfully there is a lot more to his life and this book.<br /><br />The heart of this book is not the founding of, or his history with, The Rolling Stones. The heart of the book, and his life, is his love of music, and the joy it brings to him.  It is amazing to see how many different musicians have influenced him and just how many he has played with over the years.  It literally is a Who's Who of music, from Howlin' Wolf to Willie Nelson.  It is also about family, friends, some frank discussions on drugs, and life in general.<br /><br />Here is a *nugget from Keith, for you guitar players out there: Most of the Rolling Stones guitar parts are tuned to Open G - usually Banjo tuning.<br /><br />The way this book flows, it feels as if you aren't reading a book.  It feels more like sitting down with Richards and listening to him and his friends tell you the story as they go along; it has a stream of consciousness feel to it.  You are drawn in.  You come away feeling like you have known him for years.<br /><br />The major issue with this book is a minor one.  It has nothing to do with the language, well kind of.  I really wish the publisher would have put in a glossary of the British slang used in the book.  I was literally sitting near a computer as I read this book looking up different words and sayings, so I could understand what he meant.  One example is the word \\"pong.\\"  In America it is the name of a classic video game, but in Britain it means an unpleasant smell.<br /><br />When Keith and the Stones first came over to the States to tour, he noticed they were more accepted by Blacks than by Whites, who looked at them as completely alien.  Throughout his life, you notice how he is able to straddle both worlds, but at times feels more at home in the old juke joints.<br /><br />When Mick Jagger got knighted, Keith couldn't understand how Mick could sell out and accept an honor from the same people who tried to put them in jail.<br /><br />One of his well know nicknames \\"Keef,\\" we find out, didn't come from the fans but from his family - starting out as \\"Cousin Beef\\" and morphing to \\"Keef.\\"<br /><br />Among the quotes that stick out to me as being extremely insightful:<br /><br />\\"But leaving a newborn is something I can't forgive myself for.  It's as if I deserted my post.\\" Pg  385<br /><br />That was one of the most jarring quotes I came upon. It was about the death of his new born son, Tara.  He was away in Paris, on Tour with his older son Marlon, when he got the news that Tara died of Crib death.  That is one thing no father or mother ever wants to experience.  I can't imagine the hell he went through, after that.<br /><br />As men, we are called to look after our families, and Keith was out working to provide for his family, when Tara died.  We are called to man our posts and to never forsake or abandon them.  I don't think Keith did, but I totally understand why he feels that way.<br /><br />Life by Keith Richards reminds us that life is never as cut and dry as we sometimes make it out to be. 006112429X|5.0|The title and cover art for We Need To Talk About Kevin do not do this book justice. To me, they make the book seem like a beach read or chick-lit.  The book is anything but.  WNTTAK was an amazingly well written book that makes honest psychological observations about the nature of motherhood, the existence of pure evil, and the old debate of nature v. nuture. And it does a masterful job of leaving the reader left to his or her own devices about forming a conclusions based on those observations.      Not only does Shriver give the reader a suspensful and insightful plot, this is really a \\"reader's reader\\" book.  Her phrasing and word choice are deliciously \\"meta.\\"  Follow, for instance, the evolution of usage of the word \\"trite\\" through the story. [Beginning with defining it to young Kevin, to using it to describe the very action the book is based on, to books dealing with school killings in general]. 034552554X|5.0|At the opening of the novel, Victoria Jones is eighteen, and therefore, no longer a ward of the state. To begin her life, she is given three months temporary housing and twenty dollars by the social worker who has handled her foster case for years. With that money, Victoria buys five gallons of milk, drinks each gallon, and cuts the containers in half to start a garden in her room.    It is at this early point in the story, when Victoria's fingernails are dirty from stealing plants from suburban yards and cultivating them in the transitional home where she resides, that I fell in love with her. As the novel progressed, each chapter deepened and reaffirmed my commitment to her-starting with her past in cruel foster homes, touching her profound understanding of plants and what they mean, and moving through her times of peace, love, and also, terrible pain. Throughout the book her character achieves a true, gradual, and believable transformation.    The book moves back and forth from Victoria's past to the present, and each character is rendered with dignity and empathy. Diffenbaugh weaves the characters and times together with the beauty and elegance of a well made bouquet, and presents them to her readers in a moving and powerful debut novel.    Reminiscent of WHITE OLEANDER but wholly original, THE LANGUAGE OF FLOWERS is sure to be a bestseller, and is one of my favorite books of the summer.

B0053VKP8S|5.0|I bought this thing in keeping with the ethos of my speaker system: a chain of insane-bang-for-the-buck products. Cheap to the point of painfully cheap, but nothing is low-quality.     This means:    Lepai Tripath TA2020A+ (TA-2020 chip T-Amp)  Fostex FE206e-based speakers   Bottlehead Seduction tube phono preamp (DIY kit)  Music Hall MMF-5 turntable  etc.    amongst other peer-reviewed insane-value DIY interconnects, cables, etc.     And now, of course, the Fiio D3.    I was dying to try some HDtracks music, but was not keen on the whole \\"laptop headphone out\\" method of feeding it to my system. Reasoning that some of the recordings are 24bit at 192khz sample rate, I might as well find a DAC that does 192khz.     The Fiio D5 (USB version of this DAC) only goes up to 96khz. The D3 is the only one that does 192khz. Couple that with the fact that my Macbook Pro has a toslink out and it made for an easy decision, I got the Fiio D3.     I have put in past audio-based reviews on Amazon that I came from a Head-Fi.org / headphone audiophile lineage, and owned everything from my starter cans, Grado SR-80's, through Sennheiser HD-600's, on up to a pair of the magical Stax Omega II headphones paired with a Kevin Gilmore-designed custom electrostatic amp. I realized the top of the heap (or the top of one of the higher heaps, anyways) is a great place to be, but the marginal increases in absolute ENJOYMENT relative to price were just not there.     I sold all that and moved on for a few years, only to return a short time ago.     Since then, and you may laugh, I wanted to go a lot more portable and a LOT cheaper. Bang for the buck, in other words. I wanted no separate headphone amp (an audiophile sin, I know, but my strong choice), just a pair of stock cans and a stock mp3 player to enjoy as-is. By reading reviews and careful purchasing and testing, I have found that for day to use, I really dig and enjoy the Sennheiser sound as expressed through the better-sounding-than-iPod Sansa Clip+ and Sennheiser PX-100 headphones playing FLAC audio files. It has huge natural soundstaging, is very much alive and yet flatters lower-quality recordings, making just about all music sound GREAT (even pop) without sacrificing NATURAL and living detail. It is a fun, rewarding portable rig.     Anyhow, I also took the same tack at home, building the DIY speakers mentioned above, built around a tremendously rewarding T-amp system.     So back to the Fiio D3...    I spent some quality time with the D3 last night, connected to my Macbook Pro 13\\" (mid-2010) via the very convenient toslink output that doubles as a headphone jack.     I heard that you can only get 192khz from a Macbook Pro if you boot into Windows and use some Realtek HD driver...but my chip is a Cirrus, has (according to Apple's bootcamp assistant, anyways) the very latest and best driver, and it only shows 96khz available as the maximum sampling rate! Suck. Oh well, I don't have many 192khz recordings yet, and I do have a lot of 96khz ones. For now, this thing sat at 96khz as the toslink upsample rate for the duration of this session.     I wanted to get time with it comparing it to the headphone output (approximating line-out, which you cannot truly get with the stock Macbook Pro. As usual, I had to \\"approximate\\" the line level signal, which in this case was best served by matching the rough volume of the output of the Fiio D3.     I plug this thing in and at first blush am not sure what is better: the Fiio D3 or the Macbook Pro headphone out.     The Macbook pro on first inspection had this sort of dewy-fresh, lithe, living sound that when compared to the Fiio was easier to listen to.     Putting the Fiio D3 back in, I found the sound to be orders of magnitude CLEANER: the bass of the TA2020 with my high-efficiency Fostex FE206e speakers marked a huge bass upgrade from my older TA-2024 Sonic Impact T-amp, while retaining the top-end qualities of the older amp. The Fiio D3 delivers a very separate and distinct low bass that dances independently of the upper ranges in a pleasing and natural way. The Fiio also delivers a much more \\"immediate\\" macro-sound...but loses out somewhat on micro-detail, such as space and naturalness, those audiophile intangibles that are so critical to the ILLUSION we seek, which is BELIEF.     (By the way, I stopped listening to audiophile geeks in forums long ago...terminology, hair-splitting subtleties and tense listening are what they specialize in...I have come to realize after doing that myself for quite a while, that the important thing is \\"how does it make you FEEL?\\" Do you feel like the performer is in the room? The band? Etc.)    The D3, in my opinion, initially suffers from a pretty bad case of \\"digitalitis.\\" You know, where certain female voices, violins, snare drums, etc. just reach out at you a little too aggressively, with too little refinement and air? In other words, fatiguing sound?    After a few hours of listening (or perhaps ear-deadening!) I noticed that particularly low treble band started to smooth out. Leaving it run overnight (and getting a \\"fresh set of ears\\") confirmed that burn-in is noticeable with this unit, so give it a day to run before you do your more critical listening. Not perfect but better.     The sound is frustratingly veiled. I should NOT be complaining at this price, and I am not. But it is SO good at sounding \\"WHOA, LIVE!\\" but never quite gets to sounding \\"WHOA, ALIVE!\\" It suffers from that \\"Really Good Concert PA\\" sound...you know it's there in the room with you, but you don't believe in it entirely. There is \\"system sound\\" to it, not exquisite, natural and live sound. Acoustically recorded albums are presented gorgeously, but there are either tweaks in my system that need to be done, or there is just some sort of frustratingly insurmountable felt-like veil to the sound. You KNOW there is living, breathing music behind it, your mind says so, and the high-quality overall sound and macro-detail make it obvious, but just for some reason those fine, fine details, the air, the space in the room, etc. are being muffled.     Don't get me wrong: for the price (or any price up to $200-300) this thing is amazing in it's class. I am exaggerating some of the downsides (for example, drums can sound very real and immediate on this thing, big, bold and real-drum-kit-sized in your room...they often do in mine with the Fiio D3. Only the cymbals really suffer much from lack of air around them, lack of absolute resolution, etc.) For twenty-eight bucks it's a no brainer if you were not going to spend any more anyways.     As for possible solutions...    The RCA outputs are cheap-looking, but it's no matter: I am probably going to remove the cheapo RCA's and hard-wire some Cat5 or other interconnects into it and use the holes left as their portals of exit. At this cheap, who cares.     Obviously my goal is to minimize mechanical and maximize hardwired/soldered connections. It's the cheap but inconvenient way to get halfway to \\"high end interconnects\\"...nothing more pure than a direct mechanical soldered connection!    Anyhow, this thing is very much in the \\"Grado\\" camp - detail to a fault. I spent years with my headphone rigs listening mostly to 60's tiki records, because they were so gorgeously recorded and presented on all my rigs. But I do love pop. And rock. And all that \\"fun\\" and - yes - modern music.     When your SYSTEM (and not your TASTES) is forcing you to listen to a certain kind of record or certain kind of music, I think that is a problem. A big problem. A really big problem. A huge...well, you get where I am going with this.    If I like Tears for Fears' first record, or if I dig Cee-Lo Green's latest or Junior Senior or Lykke Li or Alice in Chains...I want my system to present them in a flattering way, just as flattering as that acoustically recorded jazz record or that godforsakenly cheesy Tiki lounge record from the 60's. Systems do exist which DO this. Yours should as well. \\"Warts and all\\" is just a ridiculous attachment to \\"that one time I heard more detail from this one album, therefore I am into the 'warts and all' sound.\\" That is not music, and now how music should be reproduced. That Alice In Chains record sounded awesome when it was being recorded...not a squealy screetching grinding mess like it does on gear that suffers from peaky performance or non-aesthetic distortions.     Just like the above example, there is penty of art to be had in pop...yet some systems (cough::grado::cough) just DO NOT present it well. It's fatiguing and a slog through a marathon, versus fun, natural and beautiful.     I always laugh when people say Sennheiser headphones are \\"dark\\" or are not good for pop and rock. Au contraire, mon frere: you have not heard pop until you have cranked it up (because you can do that without wanting to scream from ear pain) on a pair of Sennheisers.     This presentation is more Grado-like: it has detail, but there is too much. Treble is shouty in bands, mainly. It has a TON going for it...midrange can be a touch flat, and this definitely has a \\"solid state\\" sound signature: literal, erring towards too much of things (versus a tube-like \\"a touch shy in parts\\"...which I prefer, all told).    Anyways, this thing is definitely worth $200-300 IMO, made a noticeable and large improvement in the immediacy, depth and live-ness (if not alive-ness) of sound and thus it will stay in my system.

1427210497|2.0|Unlike many other reviewers here, I never read Franzen's Corrections, and after reading Freedom, I'm not sure I ever will. At it's core, Freedom is novel of enormous length & wordiness, but it truly has very little to say. With the exception of perhaps Jessica, the main characters are driven by a pathological desire to make themselves and everyone around them miserable. Walter, in his sparkling goodness, may be the character who generates the widest orbit of misery. He is as lost and misguided as the rest of the cast, but he's such a 98-lb weakling, that you want him to stand up and fight back. Sadly, when he finally does, I couldn't help but think of someone like Woody Allen getting upset and going medieval. Even his rant sounds like nerdy whining. Patti is an idiot. Richard is perhaps the most interesting character in the book, but in his stereotypical rockerness, he fills too convenient of a mold to find solid footing. The entire novel slides fitfully back and forth in a rut of sexual disappointment, sexual pursuit, thinking about disappointing sexual pursuits and descriptively repulsive sexual conversations. All of the characters are driven by it. They all ruin their lives because of it, and none of them find their conquests rewarding (except maybe Lolitha). A better editor could not have saved this book, but he or she could have saved many of us who read it from having to endure quite so much. I did find one redeeming quality, though. I listened to the audio version, and the narrarator was very good. Alas, unless you just like to hear a good reader, I implore you to take a pass on this one. You'll be happy you saved the 25 hours or so of your life.

0439023513|4.0|I started reading these books after having a great chat about books with one of my friends. I wasn't expecting much, just looking for a good read to get my mind off all the science classes I've been taking. I was shocked act how vividly descriptive Collins is with her violence. But shocked in a good way. She doesn't hold back, but neither does she become gratuitous. The violence fits the story. The description of the settings and events are just about perfect as well. A little cheesy here and there when it comes to military scenarios (my fault for being in the military, it spoils books and movies all the time), but overall her ability to use details to move the story forward give her books a brisk, easy (not unintelligent, just not difficult) pace. However, her character development outside of her three main characters (Katniss, Peeta, and Gale) is pretty week. I have always thought that first person perspective in literature is the weakest way to tell a story. It's so limited by, obviously, one perspective. It works on some levels, but not on others. I believe is why Kat's character is so passive all the time. This allows other characters to have more of an effect on her. It works most of the time, since circumstances in the story rely on her to react more often than simply act. If there wasn't a war, a Hunger Games, or a family to feed, Kat would be another rank and file citizen of her district. Kat really isn't an exciting person, she can just do exciting things in right situations. But enough about Kat, there were moments in the other books that offered perfect opportunities for characters to express themselves allowing the readers to learn more about the people they've gone through hell with. But no, Collins paraphrases dramatic moments for some apparent reason. I don't want to say laziness because obviously she isn't lazy (award winning novelist!), but I don't get it. Conversations with Haymitch, Prim, Kat's mom, all could have gone along way to give thesesupporting characters some life. Which could have made these books masterpieces instead of just really good reads.      Still I did absolutely love the ending. It was perfectly done so that it wasn't sappy but still gave hope that the characters will move on with their lives and find some measure of happiness. And as anyone who has tragedy strike their lives knows, the pain and memories never really fade, they just get easier to deal with. And hopefully they don't change you so much that you become a monster. That's a hard concept for some writers to grasp, but Collins does it with ease. So, in the end I am glad I read this series, but it definitely does not impact my life the way some books have. Still, fours stars Collins. The movies better be R though, the content deserves to be preserved, otherwise it will lose it's context.

0807014273|5.0|Frankl's narrative brings me face to face with Nazi concentration camp: the abhorring degradation of fellow human beings, mass murdering, barbaric treatment of prisoners. Yet, human souls were able to seek comfort in natural beauty in such unbearable existence. Ultimately the book is about triumph, particularly spiritual triumph despite all the suffering one went through. If a person can emerge from such experience: being stripped naked, taken away all possessing, being beaten and starved, doing harsh labor in the bitter winter, facing arbitrary death in every moment, later learned that his parents and wife were all killed in gas chamber, but coming out compassionate, forgiving and becoming a successful doctor and author (publishing 30 books afterwards), how can we not marvel at human resilience?<br /><br />The eventual survival of Frankl may be due to luck (not boarding the last train), his own social smart (avoiding offending prison guards, befriending the foremen), and kindness from fellow prisoners (extra bread saved, joint escape attempt). But it also crucially depended on his spiritual strength and holding on to the final hope. He called this search of \\"meaning of suffering\\". By assigning meaning to his suffering,  he then would not fall prey to bitter disappointment when liberation did come at an expected date, while a fellow prisoner lost hope and died. In other words, Frankl found a way to maintain eternal hope. It is this hope enables him to stand strong despite any suffering or disappointment.<br /><br />In the last chapter,  Frankl offered an invaluable explanation on logotherapy. Its methodology is enlightening, much similar to what Anthony Robbins has preached all along in his seminar. Humans need \\"meaningful goals\\" to feel happy.<br /><br />Each of us goes through life with unique experience. No one can repeat that experience for us. Even our suffering is unique. Thus the meaning of our life is put upon us instead of us searching for it. It is how we answer life, in our unique way.<br /><br />The bare truth of concentration camp, the honesty and the compassion deeply touches me. There is no self pity or hatred. Instead you experience a deep sense of beauty in this writing. All you feel is a much larger understanding for human existence and the ultimate triumph of human spirit.<br /><br />How can we feel despair when men endured and survived concentration camp? Even if the world problem is daunting, Frankl said, \\"the world may be in a bad state, but everything will become worse unless each of us does his best\\". As horrible as Nazi concentration camp is, Frankl lived to tell the story, to share his experience. All those who died did not die in vain. Ultimately goodness and justice, and human spirits triumph in this world.nique way.     The bare truth of concentration camp, the honesty and the compassion deeply touches me. First off, the music on this concert CD consists of old-school blues played in a jazz/Dixieland style. While I wasn't familiar with many of the songs, the interpretations really work and allow all of the various instruments to be showcased at one time or another - trumpet, clarinet, piano, banjo, trombone, drums. Every now and then you can hear a Clapton electric guitar riff and only once or twice does it seem out of place. Layla is reworked as a Dixieland dirge with a plodding tempo and, man does it work that way.     You can tell right away that these guys are having a BLAST. It really shows in the playing, which is masterful. And effortless. At times you think these guys just hooked up for a jam session, and then you listen to some unison/harmonized horn runs and realize the technical proficiency on display. Not one note is out of place.    In addition, this is a really hot recording. It seems like microphones were placed midway between the crowd and the band and turned all the way up so every little sound in the room is picked up. It really makes you feel imbedded in the concert and adds to the feel. And the acoustic horn and percussion instruments blend together exceptionally. It reminds me of being in Preservation Hall in New Orleans.    So all I can say is that the musicianship on here is incredible and the CD is a lot of fun. If you like Dixieland-based jazz arrangements, you will really enjoy this.

His book uses the story of TOMS his successful for-profit shoe company as a blueprint to show and inspire others to start something that matters.<br />While traveling in Argentina (South America) in 2006 Blake met a woman who belonged to an organization that collected shoes from donors to give to impoverished kids. When Blake witnessed the blisters, sores and infections on the children's bare feet the idea came to him to create a for-profit shoe company with a social mission. He created a new version of \\"alpargata\\" Argentina's comfortable, casual, canvas shoe and for every pair of shoes his company sold he pledged to donate a pair of shoes to a needy child. He named his company TOMS (a promise for a better tomorrow). Blake has fulfilled his promise. TOMS has given more than a million pairs of shoes to children in need the past five years.<br />There are seven themes in Blake's self-help book: find your story, face your fears, be resourceful without resources, keep it simple, build trust, giving is good business and the final step which is moving from thinking about doing something to actually doing it.<br />To find your passion and mission in life Blake offers three questions: \\"If you did not have to<br /> worry about money, what would you do with your time? What kind of work do you want to do? What cause would you like to serve?\\"<br />Blake's story is changing the definition of success. He says the quest for success is not the same as the quest for status and money. The definition has broadened to include contributing something to the world. He says TOMS is a success precisely because they created a new model. The giving component of TOMS makes their shoes more than a product. They're part of a story, a mission and a movement anyone can join.<br />Blake also shares the stories of other people who started something that matters. Their stories show how we can make a living, have the time and freedom to do the things we love and contribute to making the world a better place.n make a living, have the time and freedom to do the things we love and contribute to making the world a better place.

B003ZSJ212|1.0|In 1977 my brother and I, along with a couple of friends went across the bay to the Coronet theater to see Star Wars.  I was unimpressed at the time, having seen a couple of the silly trailers.  As we got closer to the theater - still some blocks away - I noticed these looooong lines, and discovered that they went off in 2 directions, and ended (or began) at the theater, and that we were going to be at the end of one of those looooong lines.  At that time I had to reevaluate the purpose of my being there.      1st - I was stranded; I either had to endure the line or find an alternate way home.     2nd - Since these people were obviously in line to see the same movie (this was in the days before the multiplex cinemas), what was so special about it that they would endure such an excruciating wait?    3rd - Did I really want to spend the rest of the day standing in line in San Francisco to see a movie that looked so silly?    As it turned out, of course I stood in line, and was shocked to discover that everybody in that line, including me, made it into the next showing, and the excitement in that theater when the lights dimmed, the curtains opened, the 20th Century Fox fanfare blared, to be followed by absolute silence, was almost unbearable.  Then the simple blue \\"a long time ago in a galaxy far, far away\\" intro, and the crowd became almost ecstatic.  Of course, then the \\"Star Wars\\" Crawl.  Keep in mind that this was NOT \\"Episode IV.\\"  It was simply called \\"Star Wars.\\"  Everybody laughed out loud at the antics of C3PO and R2D2, booed and hissed the first time they saw Darth Vader, and were totally caught up in the movie.  Many stayed to watch as the curtain finally closed, then went outside, and GOT RIGHT BACK INTO LINE AGAIN!  And, I was one of them!  Over the next 3 years I saw the movie some 20 times, and countless times since then on video or TV.    Then, Star Wars became \\"Episode IV: A New Hope.\\"  \\"Empire Strikes Back\\" came out - deeper, darker, but not as stimulating as the original.  \\"Return Of The Jedi\\" came out in 1983, which closed the book on the Luke Skywalker saga.  If Lucas had left the originals alone, maybe just tweak a scene here or there (like removing the vaseline \\"forcefield\\" effect of the speeder in the first film), then I'm sure that nobody would have minded his re-edits.  But, once he had his hand in digital effects, he altered the entire series.     Then, The Darth Vader saga was released (\\"The Phantom Menace\\", \\"Attack of The Clones,\\" and \\"Revenge Of The Sith,\\" and the whole premise of the entire franchise shifted to focus on Vader's life and redemption.  Not to mention that the last three were a shadow of the originals, and the \\"answers\\" that were provided to explain events that had unfolded in the \\"Luke\\" saga caused those of us particularly familiar with those questions (e.g. when Luke asks Leia about her mother in ROTJ, or Ben tells Luke that Luke's father wanted his son to have his lightsabre in ANH), that Lucas was just \\"mailing it in.\\"  George Lucas had abandoned his original audience in favor of a dumber group who only wanted to see battles with lightsabres and blasters, and was completely unconcerned with the story.    Still he tweaked, released the original 3 in a VHS package, then a few years ago a dvd package.  I dutifully bought those, along with the individual dvds of each of the Darth Vader saga as they were released.  I saw Han Solo reduced from the rogue character he initially was to being merely a pilot with some fighting skills.  I met a \\"younger\\" Jabba that failed to move the story along.  There was also additional backgrounds and characters, even some characters that had been replaced.  Even Darth Vader's likeness was changed.    Now, with this new package, which I've had added to my cart since it was announced, I'm being told that there are even more changes made, and none seem to be improvements.  I want to see the same movies I went to see at the theater.  Those were art - even thenewer ones still had some value.  If GL wants to make real money then he could sell the originals in all their Blu-Ray ORIGINAL glory (adding the additional footage, digital enhancements, etc as additional items), and continue to sell this package - which is what this review is supposed to be about) separately.  As it stands, I've REMOVED the set from my cart, and refuse to buy it.      Mr Lucas, I only hope you read this, but I doubt that you will.  Maybe others will - and use their wallets to make a statement.

B000PMG16U|5.0|I love The Phantom Menace. There. My secret's out.    Saying that feels like saying I love Al-Qaeda or I love NAMBLA. On the Internet, hate for George Lucas has surpassed that of Osama Bin-Laden with the recent decision to yet again alter the Star Wars Saga for the upcoming Blu-Ray release. The controversy between the Lucas \\"apologists\\" and the Star Wars \\"purists\\" brings to mind Catholic and Protestant, Muslim and Hindu, Scientist and Creationist; the same deep seated convictions stir in the hearts and minds of these two groups of fans. Never mind the global economic crisis, the revolutions taking place throughout the Islamic world, the wars in Iraq and Afghanistan, this is STAR WARS! That anyone should care so deeply about a movie is unquestionably absurd, but I find myself inexplicably drawn to this controversy. I can't help but feel angry by this hatred for a filmmaker who has given so much imagination to so many. After all, don't these people know that hatred leads to the Dark Side?    For die hard fans, no thinking person over the age of eight can enjoy The Phantom Menace. Not a year goes by that I don't come across a comic or an article taking pot shots at the prequel trilogy. The article doesn't even have to be about Star Wars yet somehow the reference is thrown out, as though we must never forget the lasting psychological wounds of so many raped childhoods. The epic failure of Episodes I, II and III has become legendary and is repeated in the media like a mantra. There is even a \\"fan\\" film depicting an 80's George Lucas being kidnapped and replaced by an evil clone, as if the \\"real\\" Lucas could never have made something as terrible as the prequel trilogy. Even suggesting that these movies might not be that bad is to be suffering from Lucas Stockholm Syndrome.    But wait. Something isn't right here. How could a movie, any movie, even one as beloved as Star Wars, spawn such hatred? It's just this kind of zealotry that gets my undies in a bunch. Since leavingthe Baptist School where I was hammered with the unerring truth of the Bible for almost a decade, I've spent my life challenging the status-quo, playing Devil's Advocate whenever prudent. For me, there is something inherently dangerous about accepting opinions as fact. The more the critics jump on the Lucas bashing bandwagon, the more I see a lynch mob.    In 1999, before The Phantom Menace hit theaters, I was not a big fan of Star Wars. I had the movies on VHS and I think I watched them once or twice. I owned none of the toys or books or posters, but the trailer for Episode I brought wonderful memories: from Thanksgiving Day, 1980, when I rode on the back of my friend's dirt bike to see The Empire Strikes Back, to the afternoon in 1983 when my sister and I waited in a line that wrapped around the theater for Return of the Jedi. Nostalgia got me excited about Episode I like no movie could; after all, it wasn't just a movie, it was a cultural event. One week before the release, I waited hours with a hundred or so people pining for the lost wonder of childhood. We listened to John Williams' symphony blasted from parked cars and relished talking about things too geeky for everyday society. I was 24 and I still felt young enough to be excited about something as trivial as a movie.    The Phantom Menace made me a kid again. I didn't look for clever dialogue or Oscar worthy performances here. I wanted spaceships and aliens and cool looking planets and awesome light-saber fights and things that went KABOOM! and Lucas delivered it in spades; I wanted all the guilty pleasures that \\"mature\\" movies couldn't offer or that my adult sensibilities never afforded me. After the film, I remember my brother liking it (not a fan), my friend and I loving it, and my four year old nephew really loving it. A Marine and fellow coworker told me, \\"Now I want to be a Jedi\\". Even the critics on Rotten Tomatoes gave it a certified 61% Fresh rating. So, at the time, there was no inkling of the hatred the movie would later spawn.    More than a decade later, a YouTube critic would say that Lucas' script for The Phantom Menace was written by an eight year old. But it's precisely that childhood exuberance and unchecked imagination that makes me love George. It's every kid's dream come true: a billion dollars in toys! How can you not enjoy watching him play? In 2005, after the credits rolled on Revenge of the Sith, something changed in me. I knew I had become more cynical, more jaded. Having a mortgage and two kids will do that to you. I knew that I would never again wait with such eager anticipation for a movie; it's one thing to enjoy a film and quite another to count the days on your calendar, wishing time could move faster. Perhaps if I had been a little older in 1999, 34 instead of 24, I might have understood the disappointment.    I have always insisted that the hatred for the prequel trilogy has more to do with aging and less to do with the actual films. Let's be honest here: Star Wars meant more to the people growing up in the 70's and 80's than any other generation. My father, who is 78, never goes to the theater anymore because they don't make good movies like The Sound of Music or Casablanca. I know a guy in his fifties who loves The Day the Earth Stood Still and Forbidden Planet, but never cared for Star Wars. I know teenagers who have never seen, or even care to watch, Star Wars. And I also know kids who adore the prequels. So is it really Lucas that's changed so much, or is it us? Is our hatred for him really a hatred for growing up? No prequel could have delivered the experience of watching the original Star Wars as a child. In the re-release, critics found flaws in the changes, but never the first cut of the films. But if we were to scrutinize every scene in the original with the same harsh treatment as the prequels, we could find much to gripe about. For instance: it never made sense to me how the Death Star suddenly appears at Yavin IV, or why, if it can move, it doesn't just fly around the moon and blow it up. The Empire Strikes Back, heralded as the best in the saga, is also the most flawed: it never seems credible that Luke was Leia's brother all along, Han could never have survived in the vacuum of that giant worm's stomach, and Yoda continuously contradicts himself when he describes the limitless power of the Force. Size matters not, but turning 900 years old, apparently, makes you useless. But in the rosy tint of childhood, the original Star Wars remains flawless. How could any movie compare with such nostalgia? With expectations bordering on religious ecstasy, the prequels were doomed to be hated, just as Indiana Jones and the Kingdom of the Crystal Skull. In contrast, casual fans seem to love the prequels, as DVD and Blu-Ray sales have proved time and again.    The Phantom Menace is far from a perfect movie, but then again, I have never seen a perfect movie. I have never accepted the notion that art should be perfect. Should writers and painters and directors think like mathematicians and diamond cutters? Should they be so careful that they never dare throw out their most unorthodox ideas lest people think them inane or offensive? I love The Phantom Menace not despite its flaws but because of them. I love that we become passionate about art, one way or another, that we find ourselves hotly debating changes to a film decades after its inception, on whether it is a crime against art or simply another expression of it; I love that we wonder whether words like YIPEE are cringe-worthy or just a sign of the times; I love that we consider racial stereotyping in an alien species; I love that we debate the physical aspect (midichlorians) of a spiritual belief system (the Force). What other movie comes close to rousing such passions? None. By a long shot.    This review reposted from [...]

All of which means that any modern remake of the film is going to have to seem just as cutting-edge as the original in a time when eye-popping computer-generated special effects have become the rule rather than the exception - no easy task given the quality of its competition.  Now that technology has caught up with \\"Tron,\\" the question must be asked, can a new version of the film still manage to outpace the technology of its own time and present something never before seen at the local cineplex?<br /><br />Well, the answer is, it doesn't quite do so, but that doesn't keep the movie from being moderately entertaining on its own terms anyway. The mood is also enhanced by the electronics-based score of Daft Punk.<br /><br />The story is essentially the same, as a human finds himself drawn into a video game and fighting for his life against the cybernetic forces that reside therein. 1572814349|5.0|When I asked to review The Golden Tarot by Kat Black, I had no idea of what was in store for me, I love reviewing decks and sharing what I think with others and generally I always give positive reviews of a deck because I feel that each deck is a true treasure for someone to cherish and develop a strong bond with. The Golden Tarot absolutely blew my mind with it's hard shelled, keepsake box and breathtakingly beautiful gilt edges and fabulous imagery! It instantly became one of my favorites.    The Golden Tarot was first introduced on the web and despite it's start involving piracy and then finally a campaign by online fans which ultimately led to it's publication by US Games Systems, we now have a gorgeous deck that brings a Medieval/Renaissance flavor that adds elegance and surprising depth to this deck. I love the stand up, slip off top box that holds the cards and the 198 page instruction booklet safe and sound between use, it's not only quite attractive but sturdy and durable. The edging on the box is done in a brown and gold-ish design that adds just the right touch to make it stand out.    This deck was created to showcase the highly under rated, phenomenal artwork from the 1200-1500's (in which we see artwork just before the very beginnings of the Renaissance period) which began to show reality rather than a stylized representation of the earlier art seen in the medieval period.  Much of the Golden Tarot's style is from a period called International Gothic that eventually evolved into the Renaissance style. Kat Black chose not to use artwork that was familiar (like works from Botticelli, daVinci and Michelangelo)  in order to bring recognition to the brilliant but lesser known paintings that needed to be shared and discovered by everyone who has the privilege of working with this gorgeous deck.    The 22 Major Arcana as well as the 56 Minor Arcana cards are all digitally collaged and I was quite surprised to discover this because the images are so seamless Iwould have never known had I not read about her teqniques and artistic style. I discussed the cards with a few friends of mine and after looking at the imagery, they were surprised as well since the cards are so perfectly presented.    The Major Arcana are absolutely magnificent, I love the earthy colors and and gilded edging on the cards that simply take my breath away, I remember remarking to folks how beautiful The Golden Tarot is with it's gilt edging and gorgeous light brown marbled borders with card titles across the bottom written in very elegant font. The deck is reminiscent of The Rider Waite and the meanings are easily recognizable with little added images that really lend a depth to readings that I haven't found in other decks, like the Temperance card in which we can see a winged nun standing in a small stream pouring water from one stylish cup to another.     Above Temperance is a small saint bestowing a blessing upon her that I absolutely love. I'm not sure why this small saint impresses me so much but it so totally fits in with this rendering of Temperance and it is one of my favorite cards from The Golden Tarot.    The Minor Arcana are also absolutely gorgeous and are also bordered with the same light brown marbled borders and titles of each card at the bottom. The suits are Wands, Coins, Swords and Cups with Court Cards named Page, Knight, Queen and King. I just adore the way Kat chose to present the Court Cards with the youthful Pages, each standing holding the symbol of their suit, the Queens each show a beautiful bust of each Queen also holding the symbol of their suit, the gallant Knights each riding an elegantly decorated steed and the King of Wands and Swords seated at a gorgeously adorned throne and the Kings of Cups and Coins standing with symbols of their suit surrounding them.    This is absolutely among one of my favorite decks and shuffles fantastically making it a joy to work with. I thoroughly enjoyed sharing my interpretations of cards pulled from The Golden tarot the last two weeks as I worked with it and got ready to write my review, I even noticed fresh ways of looking at interpretations that I may not have thought of simply because the artwork and imagery is so beautiful. I would absolutely recommend this deck for both beginners and experts alike and I truly think this would make an excellent addition to anyone's Tarot collection.

159102594X|5.0|The First Law Trilogy by Joe Abercrombie is a set of three novels adhered together to create a single, epic tale. Just like every other fantasy trilogy, I hear you shout from the sidelines. No, it is not just like every other fantasy trilogy. The novels act as progression points in developing a violent, sophisticated and politicalised world - yes, politics!<br /><br />The Blade Itself is the first novel, followed by Before They Are Hanged and Last Argument of Kings. Don't be deceived: you cannot judge these books by their covers. I quickly discovered the titles have nothing to do with the story. Though if you look carefully, you'll be able to discover the roots of these quotes and garner an appreciation of just how well-constructed these novels are. Each novel is broken into three parts, each with a quote attached as an introduction. I found that I was looking forward to the next section of the novel for both a new quote and the next stage of the tale.<br /><br />In my own opinion, the First Law Trilogy is the most well-rounded and satisfying series of novels I have ever had the pleasure of reading. Each character is interesting and believable, likeable and dislikable. The political world is intertwined with murder, violence and torture: themes that have come to define Joe Abercrombie as a serious, fantasy author. The story is not particularly exciting and jam-packed with action, but it pans out as an interesting tale that draws you in with each page turn.<br /><br />I cannot relate Joe Abercrombie to any other author I have read, or rather any other author that pulls off unjustifiable violence so well. The First Law Trilogy is a series that surprised me more often than not. I finished Last Argument of Kings with a deep sigh of satisfaction. Not surprisingly, Joe Abercrombie went on to write two more novels, Best Served Cold and The Heroes, both of which surpassed all my expectations. Abercrombie has also been commissioned to write another stand-alone novel and trilogy, set in the same world.<br /><br />The story follows three main characters and three semi-main characters. Abercrombie does well in always keeping your attention and not dawdling on one character for too long. You come to care about each character's ambitions and fears, a rare experience in a literary world competing for their reader's attention. Fantasy sells from a fan-base and Abercrombie does well in generating one.<br /><br />If you're in for a good tale of intrigue, betrayal and violence, Abercrombie is a sure bet. 1594744769|2.0|There are reasons why pictures aren't usually found in successful novels. One: readers don't typically want to be shown a picture of what characters in a novel look like. Why? As we read we develop an image of what main characters look like and we don't like our image to be contradicted with pictures of another's images (even it if said images came from the author's mind). This same concept can be applied to why a person typically favors either the book or the movie. They like the images from one better than the other and don't like the contradiction. Second: readers want to sink into a novel (especially a fantastical novel). Inserting a glob of pictures creates a jaunty book journey. Every time a reader encounters a grouping of pictures they are jaunted from the depths of the novel to the surface and it takes time (unnecessary time) to return to the depths. Riggs has not ingeniously stumbled upon some untrodden path with this idea, but, instead, clumsily thought to employ an idea that has already been tried and suffered failure. Additionally, while he has an interesting concept it's not thoroughly fleshed out. There is too much detail and time spent on the beginning of the novel which creates a boring and stagnant atmosphere. As a consequence of this, the middle to end (especially end) suffer from a speed that is too fast (a place where he could have capitalized on his concepts). I'm giving two stars because the concept was certainly unique, but it's not unique enough to balance out the major flaws of a clearly novice writer.

B002VL3R1K|5.0|My five points are based on the book's historical significance. (Review based on first English edition--1939).<br /><br />Perusal of this malevolent classic allows one to clear up many misconceptions about Nazism. For instance, we sometimes hear that the German people came to support Hitler without ever understanding his positions. MEIN KAMPF lays this notion soundly to rest!<br /><br />Some commentators doubt if National Socialism had actually been a form of socialism. It is therefore instructive that Hitler supported trade unions (pp. 874-876), and that the annotators of MEIN KAMPF recognized the fact that the Nazi economy had been a Planswirtschaft (planned economy; p. 880).<br /><br />Traditional Christian theology, about Jews and deicide, is often blamed for the Holocaust. We learn instead that Hitler's pathological hatred of Jews had NOTHING to do with Christian theology. In fact, the only time that Hitler even touched on this issue was when he asserted that Jews crucified Christ for having opposed their commercialization of the Temple (p. 423). Indeed, Hitler saw Jews as a parasitic element that takes the hard-earned wealth of others. They did this through such things as becoming intermediaries in trade, and through usury (pp. 419-on). When the masses rebel against this exploitation (as through pogroms), the Jews ingratiate themselves to the rulers for protection. No matter how many times they are persecuted, Jews will always return to their ingrained exploitative ways (p. 427). Furthermore, Jews will, according to Hitler, resort to all forms of mendacity and slander in order to protect their privileged, exploitative positions. Such things as Jewish assimilation, philanthropy, and conversions to Christianity are nothing but disguises to further Jewish ends. Hitler was unfazed by the fact that the PROTOCOLS OF THE ELDERS OF ZION were inauthentic. To him, the Jewish reaction against this publication was itself proof of its authenticity (p. 424). Finally, whether themselves authentic or not, the PROTOCOLS were supposed to constitute a valid appraisal of Jewish attitudes and goals.<br /><br />Hitler unambiguously believed that only Germanic peoples invent things (pp. 397-398). The creativity of a society is directly proportional to the Germanic content of its stock and inversely proportional to the degree of \\"bastardization\\" with inferior races. The Fuhrer made countless racist statements against Slavs. Hitler repeatedly lamented the fact that the admixture of Jews and Slavs had polluted the Germanic element of his native Austria (e. g., pp. 138-140). The Fuhrer also contended that Slavs have no capability of self-government--their rulers must all be of Germanic origin (p. 951). Hitler was no white supremacist; he was a German supremacist par excellence!<br /><br />Proponents of Holocaust uniqueness sometimes claim that, whereas there was nothing that Jews could do to change their standing in the eyes of the Nazis, Poles could redeem themselves by undergoing Germanization. This is fallacious. Hitler said, \\"Here too one believed that one could bring about a Germanization of the Polish element by a purely linguistic integration into the German nationality. Here too the result would have been an unfortunate one: people of an alien race, expressing its alien thoughts in the German language, compromising the height and the dignity of our own nationality by its own inferiority.\\" (p. 590). (During the later German occupation of Poland, the \\"Germanization\\" of selected Poles was actually a re-Germanization of ethnic Germans who had previously become Polonized.)<br /><br />We sometimes hear the claim that the German people voted for Hitler, in free elections, without realizing the fact that he would start a major war. Perhaps, some say, Hitler would be, at worst, content with a little war to rectify the \\"injustices\\" of Versailles. In actuality, there is much more to MEIN KAMPF than the customary German self-pity regarding WWI and Versailles. Clear proof that the Fuhrer had long planned a war of massive conquest and extermination, against the Slavic peoples, is provided by his following statements regarding lebensraum: \\"Neither eastern nor western orientation should be the future goal of our foreign policy, but an eastern policy signifying the acquisition of the necessary soil for our German people.\\" (pp. 965-966). (Later, the Nazis spoke of \\"resettlement\\" of Jews and Slavs. In time, \\"resettlement\\" evolved into a euphemism for extermination.)ng planned a war of massive conquest and extermination, against the Slavic peoples, is provided by his following statements regarding lebensraum: \\"Neither eastern nor western orientation should be the future goal of our foreign policy, but an eastern policy signifying the acquisition of the necessary soil for our German people.\\" (pp. 965-966). I decided, too, that a device that either slipped into the cassette slot or plugged into the cigarette lighter plug would be the most convenient. So I started my on-line shopping hunt for my options. I found a number of candidate devices. Lots of super cheap options via eBay and a choice or two here on Amazon.    Many of these devices were so cheap that I figured I would try the \\"buy a few and at least one may work okay\\" strategy. Honestly, at first I ignored the Satechi Soundfly AUX because of its price.    After getting a few \\"cheap-o\\" devices in, I had confirmed the old saying, \\"You get what you pay for.\\" One broke and left hard-to-retrieve bits in the cigarette lighter and blew a fuse. One cassette device worked okay -- it was the only one I found that could work in the cassette width-wise slot I have -- but it had a built-in battery requiring that you eject and manually turn it on and off. One of the rather cheap cigarette lighter ones worked okay, but its very small LCD display showed only the \\"raw\\" folder and file names (8-char shortened and usually hidden) rather than the full MP3 album names and song titles.    So, realizing that, well, cheap is cheap, I decided to do a second round of trials. That is when I took a closer look at the Soundfly AUX.    Yes,it was the most expensive of the players I now have. But most expensive in this case is relative. Compared to ripping out a good radio/cassette and replacing it with a modern system, the Soundfly is a bargain!    Delivery once ordered was blazingly fast. A++ to the Satechi folks for that. My AUX also came in retail \\"display-worthy\\" plastic-protective packaging. And, believe it or not, it actually had  reasonable printed documentation. (Sounds funny, but a number of the items I bought came from China and likely had no packaging and printed material because they were not intended for USA retail sales.)    So, having the info I needed to install and configure the FM pre-sets, the digital 'equalizer', etc., it was fast and simple to get the Soundfly AUX in and working. The digital display has large and easily read \\"ticker-tape\\" style scrolling text for the ablum and song titles. The credit card small remote works great. All in all, a super \\"keeper.\\" Also, and its a small feature, the SD-card has a spring load socket and the card goes well into the device so it is not sticking out and subject to loss like some of the other devices I bought.    So, bottom line, I now have a BOATLOAD of great tunes on a 16 GB SD-card (not included) in my Soundfly which is playing rock-solid through my \\"new\\" old car's sound system. BOTH the Satechi folks and their AUX product are excellent.    Thanks, Satechi. Keep up the good work.

He's a pedantic, gloomy, mundane little man with a methodic, analytical mind and a keen eye for detail.  He seems more like a beleaguered civic bureaucrat than a spy master.<br /><br />The plot: Smiley has been forced into retirement, cast aside to make way for the new guard in the wake of a disastrous mission that results in a major shift in power at the Circus, the British Secret Service Headquarters.  He is drawn out of retirement, unofficially, to investigate whether or not there is a mole at the highest levels of the Service.  The suspects have been code named Tinker, Tailor, Soldier, Poorman and Beggarman.<br /><br />Le Carre is in no rush to unravel his tale, allowing the story to advance at a slow measured pace.  There are no cliffs to hang from.  Smiley delves into his own memories, pores through files, conducts interviews and gradually draws details from reluctant former colleagues.  He doesn't insult the reader's intelligence by providing too much detail, requiring the reader to figure much of what is happening out on their own.<br /><br />No spoon feeding here.<br /><br />The story itself is loosely based on actual events and is...well...complicated.  There are numerous players in the puzzle and a certain degree of commitment is required on the part of the reader to keep all the characters, subplots, and flashbacks straight.  In the end, it is a rewarding read and provides fascinating insight into Cold War era espionage.  As a novel though, I never felt fully engaged in the story.  It was an interesting novel, but I never really connected with it on any emotional level.  I was curious to see how the puzzle would be solved, but at the same time, I didn't feel like I was invested in the outcome.<br /><br />This is a smart novel that's well worth reading.  I appreciated its complexity and its insiders knowledge of the spy game even if I wasn't fully engaged by the characters and the story.ested in the outcome. B000065PUE|5.0|No one who listened to the raw, churning, aggressive guitar melody of &#34;Levitate Me&#34;, the first track the Pixies ever recorded (and the last cut on their first release, &#34;Come On Pilgrim&#34;) could have mistaken it for a mutated sort of post-punk pop song or predicted that it would serve as a template for the quiescent/aggressive musical dynamic informing the band's iconic sound. And yet with the benefit of 25 years of hindsight, both statements seem pretty accurate to me. &#34;Doolittle&#34;, the band's third release, was the apotheosis of this juxtaposition between tuneful discordancy and an undeniable melodicism underlying  hooks large enough to hang Black Francis's Size XX flannel shirts upon, and in my opinion, the finest post-punk pop release to have been recorded, period.  Most of the tracks the Pixies recorded for &#34;Come On Pilgrim&#34; and &#34;Surfer Rosa&#34;, though quiescent in some parts, could be defined as possessing a sense of ferocious abandon, were often aggressively paced and frequently built toward a sense of sustained explosiveness.  And you'll still hear that on &#34;Doolittle&#34;, most notably on &#34;Tame&#34; , `Wave of Mutilation&#34;,&#34;I Bleed&#34;, &#34;Monkey Gone To Heaven&#34;, and &#34;Crackity Jones&#34;. Yet often an acoustic guitar could be heard complementing a bristling electric lead (first heard most prominently on &#34;Nimrod's Son&#34; from &#34;Come On  Pilgrim&#34;). &#34;Doolitle&#34; is the first CD where the quiescent/aggressive dynamic is built solidly from this mixture of electro-acoustical guitar interplay  (their breakout song &#34;Here Comes Your Man&#34;, revolves around this interplay), reaching its zenith on &#34;La La Love You&#34; and &#34;No. 13 Baby&#34;, leaving no doubt they were the spiritual descendants of Husker Du by way of Peter, Paul, and Mary.  Some reviewers have criticized Francis's lyrics for opaqueness and superficiality. But to me, it's precisely those lyrics that at first glance seem cryptic- yet containing just a tiny kernel of logic that could be deciphered- that added that extra amplification to the immediacy and urgency of the sound behind it. Significantly deviant behaviors (the real-life over-the-cliff-and-into-the-ocean vehicular suicides that inspired &#34;Wave Of Mutilation&#34;, the exhibitionism displayed by the &#34;six-foot girl&#34; in &#34;No. 13 Baby) rubs shoulders with  the spiritual (&#34;Mr. Grieves&#34;, perhaps drawn from Francis's Evangelical Christian upbringing, seems like an updated version of the old hymn &#34;Man of Sorrows&#34; ;&#34;Monkey Gone To Heaven&#34;, a severe indictment of the destruction humankind has wrought during their time spent in stewardship of the environment, juxtaposes the evolutionary fixation of mankind's supposed origins with the ascencion toward a Supreme Being after death for judgment; and both &#34;Dead&#34; a cryptical revision of David's dalliance with Bathsheba  and &#34;Gouge Away&#34; ,  a quirky re-telling of the  story of Samson and Delilah, are derived straight from the Old Testament), which brushes against the existential  &#34;I Bleed&#34;, with its universal query, &#34;Prithee my dear/why are we here?&#34;  that clicked with the macabre &#34;(Debaser&#34;, in which the slicing of eyeballs serves as a metaphor for the degradation of society through the pornographic display of sex and violence , as well as &#34;Crackity Jones&#34;, in which the song's protagonist is attempting to escape from a potentially homicidal agoraphobe). And nearly all of it is yelped, yowled, yodeled, whispered, or growled in Francis's Pentecostal roar, with Kim Deal's warm, husky vocals providing either harmony or counterpoint (and taking lead on &#34;Silver&#34;). David Lovering, the band's propulsive percussionist, sings lead on &#34;La La Love You&#34;, and is backed by the rest of his bandmates, giving his rich, expressive vocals a chance to serve as a focal point for once.  My review of &#34;Doolittle&#34; is the 262nd to have been added to this page, and you're probably encountering it because as of the date of its posting, it's the most recent review. Many other reviewers have stressed the influence of &#34;Doolittle&#34; as an ancestral link to the grunge movement, but I think it's a landmark recording because it incorporates significant features that are universally associated with a typical pop song with the more aggressive, discordant edge characteristic of post-punk music. Some of the rough edges have been polished away by the production values of Gil Norton, but it's only served to make &#34;Doolittle&#34; the most cohesive of the Pixies' releases; there's literally not a track without some kind of interesting wrinkle on it, and it's as compelling to listen to your thousand-and-first time as it is your first. I can't recommend it enough.

B00008YJHC|4.0|No one I knew who listened to \\"Bossanova\\" upon its release 21 years ago was disappointed in the direction it took; for the most part, the quiescent/aggressive dynamic informing the band's sound was in plain evidence, the production values gave it a roomier and more expansive sound than \\"Doolitle\\" while still retaining an aggressive edge, Black Francis's lyrics were as cryptically poetic, and the group began incorporating the loping rhythm and reverberation of `60's surf music. And yet looking back in hindsight, \\"Bossanova\\" marks a transitional point in collaborative effort for the Pixies, one that wasn't necessarily beneficial; Black Francis asserted much more control over the material and somewhat diminished the active role Kim Deal had assumed during the rehearsals and recording for \\"Come On Pilgrim\\", \\"Surfer Rosa\\", and \\"Doolittle\\", eventually laying the ground for the dissolution of the Pixies.     This is evident in the level of participation in the group's dynamics allowed to her beyond laying down heavily pronounced basslines as one-half of the group's percussion section. \\"Silver\\" and Gigantic\\" had been her compositional contributions to \\"Doolittle\\" and \\"Surfer Rosa\\" respectively, but she was never given the chance to contribute material to \\"Bossanova\\", instead relegated to providing backing vocals for a bare minimum of tracks, including \\"Velouria\\", \\"Dig For Fire\\", and \\"Havalina\\", which is a shame, as her vocal chemistry with Black Francis was remarkable.     And while Black Francis's lyrics remained both cryptic and (at a certain level) spiritually profound, many of them revolve around topics some might be inclined to universally associate with science fiction. \\"Velouria\\", driven by raw, churning, somewhat aggressive guitar intersected by a tremolo, is about a time-traveler pursuing a love affair after his possible emergence through a dimensional portal to an alternate universe. \\"Ana\\", driven by brushed drums and a wonderfully reverbed guitar line, relates the tale of a gigantic alien surfer. Both driven by piano and heavily distorted gutiar, \\"The Happening\\" attempts to predict the reaction to a visible landing of an unidentified flying object. \\"All Over The World\\" introduces fish who apparently have the universal capacity for articulate speech, while a martial stomp and a punishingly aggressive, wriggling, angular guitar lead propels \\"Stormy Weather\\", which might very well be referring to some unnamed cataclysmic event so devastating that only one line (\\"It is time for stormy weather') comprises the lyrical content of the entire song.     \\"Bossanova\\" has more than enough to recommend it; though recorded as it was being written, it sounds fairly cohesive, and aside from \\"Velouria\\" and \\"The Happening\\", \\"Is She Weird\\" , \\"Dig For Fire\\", and \\"Stormy Weather\\" have all become an integral part of their canon. It's worn well with the passage of over two decades, and sounds fresh next to the death-laughs-forever, world-won't -listen, fashionably alienated heavy metal that's busily masquerading as \\"alternative rock\\" 21 years after its release. It's definitely worth grabbing, but do it in chronological order of release. Recommended.

0767827902|5.0|According to director Peter Bogdanovich, he made this movie on location in Archer City, Texas in 2 months and spent another 6 months editing.  By his own admission, he didn't know many of the technical aspects of filmmaking so reworking the prints was necessary.  In the end, he got it all right.  Shot in glorious black and white in order to promote the grim, bleak decay of a small Texas town, the film also gives us a complex set of characters.  With the exception of Best Supporting Actor winner Ben Johnson, the cast was mostly unknown actors at the time.  This is model Cybill Shepherd's first role.  Bogdanovich shot the film using traditional camera techniques.  In addition to an established color pallet, most films of the era were being produced by directors experimenting with hand held cameras, quick cuts and special effects.  \\"Picture Show\\" exhibits like the era it portrays, 1951-1952 dusty Texas.  Bogdanovich doesn't use a musical score.  Instead he uses the music of the day with an emphasis on Hank Williams.  In fact a couple shots feature an old 45 rpm changer and of course a jukebox.  By my count there are 8 or 9 central characters and Bogdanovich manages to flesh out each one so we know who they are and what they represent.  For the most part the central focus is on Sonny (Timothy Bottoms) a graduating high school senior who comes to realize, life isn't getting any better, but seemingly has no options.  The film was controversial at the time.  It showed a change in American demographics as people were leaving the small towns that dotted the country for bigger cities that had more opportunities.  \\"The Last Picture Show\\" is replete with nudity, adultery and sex.  While this was not unheard of at the time, the context of it being in the lives of regular small town citizens was.  The screenplay was adapted from Larry McMurtry's book of the same name.  He grew up in Archer City, so I suspect all the events really happened, even if the characters may have differed.  The film was hailed by critics at the time but lost the Best Picture Oscar to \\"The French Connection\\".  With this his second film Peter Bogdanovich solidified his place in film history with this brilliant masterpiece.. 0679746323|5.0|After exhaustive documental, bibliographic, testimonial, and iconographic research, the authors put together the puzzle of this personality, one of the defining characters of the XX Century, although only of its miseries, and not of its wonders. A century of excess, which saw the greatest scientific advances of history, as well as enormous changes in society and the economy, but which also saw Humankind's descent into the deepest abyss of degradation, injustice, violence, and cruelty. It is only this second, darker, face of the century that Mao embodies. It is impossible to think of a human being - Hitler and Stalin included - who ever inflicted more damage, in a more cruel and cold way, and for so long, to more humans. In this sense, in Mao everything is big. Like most revolutionaries, subversives, despots, authocrats, and the like, Mao was never poor. He was born in a small town in the Hunan province, a child of merchants. Mao had the chance to go to school, but he never stood out. From an early age he had an overpowering ambition, as well as an absolute indifference towards any other persons. So, politics became his obvious career choice. Especially the chaotic Chinese politics of the early century, including the (very real) Japanese threat, the Russian Revolution, and the division between Nationalists and Communists, of which the peasants were nothing but victims. Into that turbulent river Mao went fishing, based on treason, simulation, and humiliation - both the abject ones he was willing to put up with, and the even worse ones he inflicted unto others. With unusual audacity and hair-raising cruelty, Mao kept changing sides until his status began to grow within the Communist Party, always more hated and feared than respected. It hurts to say this, but with the exception of two or three minor stumbles, Luck was always on his side, throughout his entire life.     His first opportunity to gather his own troops, money, territory and a reputation came in the \\"Land of Bandits\\", Jinggiang, in China's Southeast. From there, he began to build his own power-base. The second and decisive chance came with the Long March, an indeed long and absurd retreat to the North, in search of territorial contact with the Russians in order to get their help. Mao's all-powerful propaganda machine of later would transform, lie by lie, this rather embarrasing flight into a glorious and epic chapter. Then came the audacious kidnap of Chiang Kai-shek; war with Japan, in which Mao fought his internal rivals and Chiang, not the Japanese; the Soviet intervention; and at last, Mao's supremacy over the Party, thanks to a horrible mistake made by the American General Marshall, who saved him from destruction and forced Chiang into exile. From all this came the totalitarian state and a life of excess and opulence.     Up to there, the boundless ambitious had managed to conquered his vast country. Now it was time to conquer the world; this is no conjecture: he was repeatedly explicit about it. His former abjection and self-humiliation in front of Stalin became open challenge, with succesive reckless manoeuvres which nobody else would have even dreamed of to do to the fearsome Soviet boss. The biggest of them all was to provoke war in Korea, which put Stalin between a rock and a hard place. This war was a central part of Mao's ultimate goal: to become a nuclear superpower and dominate the world. To add to that end, he launched the \\"Great Leap Forward\\", which consisted in returning the Chinese to the Stone Age by exporting lots of food (and thus starving his own people) in exchange for the most sophisticated weapons systems available. Tens of millions of people died during that crazy, evil experiment, after which Mao subjected Tibet; built his awesomer propaganda machinery (aided by countless Western \\"useful fools\\"); last-minute salvation from his number-two's, Liu Shaoqui, attempted coup; the stupid and counterproductive Cultural Revolution; and finally, his personal decadence, but not the demise of his regime, which lives on.    From among the many characters in this macabre drama, four deserve special mention: Liu Shaoqui, his second in command, and Peng Dehuai,  minister of Defense, both of whom finally opened their eyes and tried to stop him, but were tortured to death; Jian Zemin, the repellent adulator and chief diplomat; and his fourth wife, Madame Mao, a former prostitute who deserves a description as a madhouse bird. There is little doubt that Mao suffered from a severe personality disorder, which made it impossible for him to perceive the least value or feelings in other people. His egomania and brutality have no possible comparison. Leaving bodies by the side of the road, right and left, betraying everybody, including his own family, and spreading death and misery, the Great Myth was created, the repugnant figure of the \\"Great Helmsman\\".    Don't get lost in minutiae about this-or-that exact source for this particular story. Get the message, which is none other than this man was a monster. Instead of trying to find something redeemable about him, think of the millions who suffered, died, and were humiliated to extremes by this horrible, vicious man. Take a look at the treatment he gave his four wives and his children. This book is a great contribution to the understanding of History and the depths of human nature.
